<div class="center" style="width:auto; margin-left:auto; margin-right:auto;">The Memorial Rooms "reflect an atmos­phere of realism", wrote a visitor, <span class="nowrap">"[as if]</span> Harry Widener still lived among his books."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-canoe_18-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canoe-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:91</sup> The desk at left was <span class="nowrap">Harry's own.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[g]</a></sup></span></div>
</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In the Memorial Rooms, after a benediction by Bishop <a href="/wiki/William_Lawrence_(bishop)" title="William Lawrence (bishop)">William Lawrence</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-transcript_8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transcript-8">[8]</a></sup> a portrait of Harry Widener was unveiled, then remarks delivered by Senator <a href="/wiki/Henry_Cabot_Lodge" title="Henry Cabot Lodge">Henry Cabot Lodge</a> (speaking on "The Meaning of a Great Library"<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-lodge_60-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lodge-60">[53]</a></sup> on behalf of Eleanor Widener) and Lowell ("For years we have longed for a library that would serve our purpose, but we never hoped to see such a library as this").<sup id="cite_ref-dedication_52-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dedication-52">[46]</a></sup> Afterward (said the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Evening_Transcript" title="Boston Evening Transcript">Boston Evening Transcript</a></i><span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span>) "the doors were thrown open, and both graduates and under­graduates had an opportu­ni­ty to see the beauties and utilities of this important univer­sity acquisition."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-transcript_8-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transcript-8">[8]</a></sup></p>
<p>"I hope it will become the heart of the University," Eleanor Widener said afterward, "a centre for all the interests that make Harvard a great university."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-alumni_19130616_collection_61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alumni_19130616_collection-61">[54]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Widener_Memorial_Rooms"><span id="memorial_rooms"></span>Widener Memorial Rooms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Widener Memorial Rooms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The central Memorial Rooms‍—‌an outer Rotunda<sup id="cite_ref-rotunda_62-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rotunda-62">[55]</a></sup> housing memorabilia of the life and death of Harry Widener,<sup id="cite_ref-HEW_history_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HEW_history-63">[56]</a></sup> and an inner Library displaying the 3300 rare books collected by him‍—‌were described by the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_Herald" title="Boston Herald">Boston Sunday Herald</a></i> soon after the dedication:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>The [Rotunda] is of <a href="/wiki/Alabama_marble" class="mw-redirect" title="Alabama marble">Alabama marble</a> except the domed ceiling, with fluted columns and <a href="/wiki/Capital_(architecture)#ionic_capital" title="Capital (architecture)">Ionic capitals</a> [while the Library] is finished in carved <a href="/wiki/Quercus_robur" title="Quercus robur">English oak</a>, the carving having been done in England; the high bookcases are fitted with glass shelves and bronze sashes, the windows are hung with heavy curtains [and] upon the desks are vases filled with flowers.</p>
<p>The big marble fireplace and the portrait of Harry Widener occupy a large portion of the south wall. Standing front of the fireplace one may look through the vista made by the doorways, the staircases within and the stairs without and get a glimpse of the green campus.<sup id="cite_ref-65" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-65">[h]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>Conversely, "even from the very entrance [of the building] one will catch a glimpse in the distance of the portrait of young Harry Widener on the further wall [of the Memorial Rooms], if the intervening doors happen to be open."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-lane_libj_48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lane_libj-48">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:325</sup></p>
<p>For many years Eleanor Widener hosted Commencement Day luncheons in the Memorial Rooms.<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:20</sup> The family underwrites their upkeep,<sup id="cite_ref-halberstam_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halberstam-66">[58]</a></sup> including weekly renewal of the flowers<sup id="cite_ref-ask_flowers_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_flowers-67">[59]</a></sup>‍—‌originally roses but now carnations.<sup id="cite_ref-69" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-69">[i]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Amenities_and_deficiencies">Amenities and deficiencies</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Amenities and deficiencies">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Touted as "the last word in library construction",<sup id="cite_ref-contract_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-contract-70">[61]</a></sup> the new building's amenities included telephones, <a href="/wiki/Pneumatic_tube" title="Pneumatic tube">pneumatic tubes</a>, book lifts and conveyors, elevators,<sup id="cite_ref-snead_6-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snead-6">[6]</a></sup> and a dining-room and kitchenette "for the ladies of the staff".<sup id="cite_ref-lane_mem_71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lane_mem-71">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:676</sup> Advertisements for the manufacturer of the building's shelving highlighted its "dark brown enamel finish, harmonizing with oak trim",<sup id="cite_ref-snead_ad_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snead_ad-72">[63]</a></sup> and special interchangeable regular and oversize shelves meant that books on a given subject could be shelved together regardless of size.<sup id="cite_ref-75" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-75">[j]</a></sup></p>
<p><i>The Library Journal</i> found "especially interesting not so much the spacious and lofty reading rooms"<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-tlj_may1915_38-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tlj_may1915-38">[33]</a></sup> as the innovation<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:255</sup> of placing student <a href="/wiki/Carrel_desk" title="Carrel desk">carrels</a> and private faculty studies directly in the stack, reflecting Lowell's desire to put "the massive resources of the stack close to the scholar's hand, reuniting books and readers in an intimacy that nineteenth-century ["closed-stack" library designs] had long precluded".<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:45-6</sup> (Competition for the seventy<sup id="cite_ref-lane_libj_48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lane_libj-48">[42]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:327</sup> coveted faculty studies has been a longstanding administrative headache.)<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[k]</a></sup></p>
<p>Nonetheless certain deficien­cies were soon noted.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:107</sup> A primitive form of air conditioning was aban­doned within a few months.<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:97</sup> "The need of better toilet facilities [in the stacks] has been pressed upon us during the past year by several rather distressing experiences," Widener Superintendent Frank Carney wrote discreetly in 1918.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[l]</a></sup> And Coolidge wrote to <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/J._P._Morgan,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="J. P. Morgan, Jr.">J. P. Morgan, Jr.</a>,</span> "There is something rather humiliating in having to proclaim to the world that we have 300 [carrels in the stacks] which furnish unequalled opportunity to the scholar and investigator who wishes to come here, but that in order to use these opportu­ni­ties he must bring his own chair, table and electric lamp." (A week later Coolidge wrote again: "Your very generous gift [has helped] pull me out of a most desperate situation.")<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1976_15-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1976-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:102</sup></p>
<p>Later-built tunnels, from the stacks level furthest underground, connect to nearby <a href="/w/index.php?title=Pusey_Library_(Harvard_University)&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Pusey Library (Harvard University) (page does not exist)">Pusey Library</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lamont_Library" title="Lamont Library">Lamont Library</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-beefs_80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-beefs-80">[68]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Library" title="Houghton Library">Houghton Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-kent_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-kent-81">[69]</a></sup> An enclosed bridge connecting to Houghton's reading room via a Widener window‍—‌built after Eleanor Widener's heirs agreed to waive<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:75</sup> her gift's proscription of exterior additions or alterations<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1976_15-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1976-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:79</sup>‍—‌was removed in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-bridge_82-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bridge-82">[70]</a></sup> (Houghton and Lamont were built in the 1940s to relieve Widener,<sup id="cite_ref-relieve_83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-relieve-83">[71]</a></sup> which had become simultaneously too small‍—‌its shelves were full<sup id="cite_ref-widener_history_84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widener_history-84">[72]</a></sup>‍—‌and too large‍—‌its immense size and complex catalog made books difficult to locate.<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[m]</a></sup> But with Harvard's collections doubling every 17 years, by 1965 Widener was again close to full,<sup id="cite_ref-deficit_86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-deficit-86">[73]</a></sup> prompting construction of Pusey.)<sup id="cite_ref-newpusey_87-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-newpusey-87">[74]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Collections_and_stacks">Collections and stacks</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Collections and stacks">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg/240px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg" width="240" height="389" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg/360px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e1/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg/480px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg 2x" data-file-width="707" data-file-height="1146" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_SneadAdvertisement_1915.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"The shelves are lost in the dark­ness above, and to either side they run off to in­fin­i­ty", wrote <a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolfe" title="Thomas Wolfe">Thomas Wolfe</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-mitchell_88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mitchell-88">[75]</a></sup> Each of the ten lev­els has some 187 rows of shelving.<sup id="cite_ref-lane_mem_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lane_mem-71">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:327</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg/240px-WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg" width="240" height="305" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg/360px-WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a9/WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg/480px-WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg 2x" data-file-width="732" data-file-height="929" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WidenerLibraryStackTier.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The two lowest stack levels before inter­vening floor panels were installed</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The ninety-unit Harvard Library system,<sup id="cite_ref-AZ_43-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:361</sup> of which Widener is the anchor, is the only academic library among the world's five "megalibraries"‍—‌Widener, the <a href="/wiki/New_York_Public_Library" title="New York Public Library">New York Public Library</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>, <a href="/wiki/Biblioth%C3%A8que_nationale_de_France" title="Bibliothèque nationale de France">France's Bibliothèque Nationale</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/British_Library" title="British Library">British Library</a><sup id="cite_ref-stam_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stam-89">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:352</sup>‍—‌making it "unambigu­ously the greatest univer­sity library in the world," in the words of a Harvard official.<sup id="cite_ref-speaking_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-speaking-90">[77]</a></sup></p>
<p>According to the Harvard College Library's own description, Widener's humanities and social sciences collections include</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>holdings in the history, literature, public affairs, and cultures of five continents. Of particular note are the collec­tions of <a href="/wiki/Africana_studies" title="Africana studies">Africana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Americana" title="Americana">Americana</a>, European <a href="/wiki/Local_history" title="Local history">local history</a>, <a href="/wiki/Judaica" class="mw-redirect" title="Judaica">Judaica</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latin_American" class="mw-redirect" title="Latin American">Latin American</a> studies, <a href="/wiki/Middle_Eastern" class="mw-redirect" title="Middle Eastern">Middle Eastern</a> studies, <a href="/wiki/Slavs" title="Slavs">Slavic</a> studies, and rich collec­tions of materials for the study of Asia, the United Kingdom and Commonwealth, France, Germany, Italy, Scandinavia, and <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Greek and Latin antiquity</a>. These collec­tions include significant holdings in <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguistics</a>, ancient and modern languages, <a href="/wiki/Folklore" title="Folklore">folklore</a>, <a href="/wiki/Economics" title="Economics">economics</a>, <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a> and technology, <a href="/wiki/Philosophy" title="Philosophy">philosophy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Psychology" title="Psychology">psychology</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sociology" title="Sociology">sociology</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[n]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>The collection's 3.5 million volumes<sup id="cite_ref-forrest_40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-forrest-40">[34]</a></sup> occupy 57 miles (92&#160;km) of shelves<sup id="cite_ref-battles_unquiet_92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battles_unquiet-92">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:4</sup> along five miles (8<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>km) of aisles,<sup id="cite_ref-fox_93-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-fox-93">[79]</a></sup> on ten levels divided into three wings each.<sup id="cite_ref-battles_unquiet_92-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battles_unquiet-92">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:4</sup></p>
<p>Alone among the "megalibraries", only Harvard allows patrons the "long-treasured privilege" of entering the general-collections stacks to browse as they please, instead of requesting books through library staff.<sup id="cite_ref-98" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-98">[o]</a></sup> Until a recent renovation the stacks had little signage‍—‌"There was the expecta­tion that if you were good enough to qualify to get into the stacks you certainly didn't need any help" (as one official put it)<sup id="cite_ref-potier_50-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potier-50">[44]</a></sup> so that "learning to [find books in] Widener was like a rite of passage, a test of manhood",<sup id="cite_ref-rolbein_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rolbein-99">[84]</a></sup> and a 1979 monograph on library design complained, "After one goes through the main doors of Harvard's Widener Library, the only visible sign says merely ENTER."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-pollet_100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pollet-100">[85]</a></sup> At times color-coded lines and shoeprints have been applied to the floors to help patrons keep their bearings.<sup id="cite_ref-bearings_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bearings-101">[86]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gewertz_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gewertz-102">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>As of 2015 some 1700 persons enter the building, and about 2800 books are checked out, daily.<sup id="cite_ref-arms_103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-arms-103">[88]</a></sup> Another 3<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million Widener items reside offsite<sup id="cite_ref-barcoding_104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barcoding-104">[89]</a></sup> (along with many millions of items from other Harvard libraries) at the <a href="/w/index.php?title=Harvard_Depository&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Harvard Depository (page does not exist)">Harvard Depository</a> in <a href="/wiki/Southborough,_Massachusetts" title="Southborough, Massachusetts">Southbor­ough, Massachu­setts</a>, from which they are retrieved overnight on request.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:170-1</sup> A project to insert <a href="/wiki/Barcode" title="Barcode">barcodes</a> into each book, begun in the late 1970s, had some 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million volumes yet to reach as of 2006.<sup id="cite_ref-barcoding_104-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-barcoding-104">[89]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Harry_Elkins_Widener_Collection">Harry Elkins Widener Collection</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Harry Elkins Widener Collection">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The works displayed in the Memorial Rooms comprise Harry Widener's collec­tion at the time of his death, "major monuments of English letters, many remarkable for their bindings and illustrations or unusual provenance":<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:9</sup> Shakespeare <a href="/wiki/First_folio" class="mw-redirect" title="First folio">first folios</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-AZ_43-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:362</sup> a copy of <i>Poems written by Wil.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>Shake-speare, gent.</i> (1640) in its original sheepskin binding;<sup id="cite_ref-cyclo_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cyclo-105">[90]</a></sup> an inscribed copy of <a href="/wiki/James_Boswell" title="James Boswell">Boswell</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/Life_of_Samuel_Johnson" title="Life of Samuel Johnson">Life of Samuel Johnson</a></i>; <a href="/wiki/Samuel_Johnson" title="Samuel Johnson">Johnson</a>'s own Bible ("used so much by its owner that several pages were worn out and Johnson copied them over in his own writing");<sup id="cite_ref-halberstam_66-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halberstam-66">[58]</a></sup> and first editions, presenta­tion copies, and similarly valuable volumes of <a href="/wiki/Robert_Louis_Stevenson" title="Robert Louis Stevenson">Robert Louis Stevenson</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Makepeace_Thackeray" title="William Makepeace Thackeray">Thackeray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Charlotte_Bront%C3%AB" title="Charlotte Brontë">Charlotte Brontë</a>, <a href="/wiki/William_Blake" title="William Blake">Blake</a>, <a href="/wiki/George_Cruikshank" title="George Cruikshank">George Cruikshank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Cruikshank" title="Isaac Cruikshank">Isaac Cruikshank</a>, <a href="/wiki/Robert_Cruikshank" class="mw-redirect" title="Robert Cruikshank">Robert Cruikshank</a><sup id="cite_ref-hewc_5-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hewc-5">[5]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Charles_Dickens" title="Charles Dickens">Dickens</a>‍—‌including the petty cash book kept by Dickens while a young law clerk.<sup id="cite_ref-hellman_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-106">[91]</a></sup> Book collector <a href="/w/index.php?title=George_Sidney_Hellman&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="George Sidney Hellman (page does not exist)">George Sidney Hellman</a>, writing soon after Harry Widener's death, observed that he was "not satisfied alone in having a rare book or a rare book inscribed by the author; it was with him a prerequisite that the volume should be in immaculate condition."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-hellman_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hellman-106">[91]</a></sup></p>
<p>Harry Widener "died suddenly, just as he was beginning to be one of the world's great collectors,"<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-alumni_19130616_collection_61-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alumni_19130616_collection-61">[54]</a></sup> said the Collection's first curator.<sup id="cite_ref-whitehill_58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitehill-58">[52]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:6</sup> "They formed a young man's library, and are to be preserved as he left it"<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-alumni_19130616_collection_61-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-alumni_19130616_collection-61">[54]</a></sup>‍—‌except that the Widener family has the exclusive privilege of adding to it.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[p]</a></sup> <span id="gutenbergbible"></span> Harvard's "greatest typographical treasure"<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-houghton_chron_108-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton_chron-108">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:17</sup> is one of the only thirty-eight perfect copies extant<sup id="cite_ref-HEW_gutenberg_109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HEW_gutenberg-109">[93]</a></sup> of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_Bible#Existing_copies_of_the_Gutenberg_Bible" title="Gutenberg Bible">Gutenberg Bible</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-houghton_gutenberg_110-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton_gutenberg-110">[94]</a></sup> purchased while Harry was abroad by his grandfather <a href="/wiki/Peter_Arrell_Brown_Widener" class="mw-redirect" title="Peter Arrell Brown Widener">Peter A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>B. Widener</a> (who had intended to surprise Harry with it once the <i>Titanic</i> docked in New York)<sup id="cite_ref-halberstam_66-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halberstam-66">[58]</a></sup> and added to the Collection by the Widener family in 1944.<sup id="cite_ref-114" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-114">[q]</a></sup></p>
<p>Like all Harvard's valuable books, works in the Widener Collec­tion may be consulted by researchers demonstrating a genuine research need.<sup id="cite_ref-widener_hewmc_115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-widener_hewmc-115">[98]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Parallel_classification_systems_and_dual_catalogs"><span id="dual_catalogs"></span>Parallel classification systems and dual catalogs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Parallel classification systems and dual catalogs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg/250px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg" width="250" height="183" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg/375px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e0/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg/500px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg 2x" data-file-width="984" data-file-height="721" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_CardCatalogs_1915.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The original catalog room, "though mag­nif­i­cent ar­chi­tec­tur­al­ly, looked [as though the catalog cases, with their 3796 drawers] had simply been dropped hap­haz­ard­ly into them."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:225</sup><sup id="cite_ref-lb_ad_116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lb_ad-116">[99]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Like many large libraries, Widener originally classified its holdings according to its own idiosyncratic system‍—‌the "Widener" (or "Harvard") system‍—‌which (writes Battles) follows "the division of knowledge in its [early twentieth-century] formulation. The <i>Aus</i> class contains books on the <a href="/wiki/Austro-Hungarian_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Austro-Hungarian Empire">Austro-Hungarian Empire</a>; the <i>Ott</i> class serves the purpose for the <a href="/wiki/Ottoman_Empire" title="Ottoman Empire">Ottoman Empire</a>. <a href="/wiki/Dante" class="mw-redirect" title="Dante">Dante</a>, <a href="/wiki/Moli%C3%A8re" title="Molière">Molière</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Montaigne" class="mw-redirect" title="Montaigne">Montaigne</a> each gets a class of his own."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-battles_unquiet_92-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-battles_unquiet-92">[78]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:15</sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s new arrivals began to be classified according to a modified version of the <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress_Classification" title="Library of Congress Classification">Library of Congress system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-wiegand_117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wiegand-117">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:256</sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:159</sup> The two systems' differences reflect "competing theories of knowl­edge<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... In a sense, the Widener system was <a href="/wiki/Aristotelianism" title="Aristotelianism">Aristotelian</a>; its divi­sions were empirical, describing and reflecting the languages and cultural origins of books and highlighting their relations to one another in language, place, and time; [the Library of Congress system], by contrast, was <a href="/wiki/Platonic_idealism" title="Platonic idealism">Platonic</a>, looking past the surface of language and nation to reflect the idealized, essential discipline in which each [item] might be said to belong."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:158-9</sup></p>
<p>Because of the impracticality of reclassifying millions of books, those received before the changeover remain under their original "Widener" classifications. Thus among works on a given subject, older books will be found at one shelf location (under a "Widener" classification) and newer ones at another (under a related Library of Congress classification).<sup id="cite_ref-movement_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-movement-118">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gewertz_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gewertz-102">[87]</a></sup></p>
<p>In addition, an accident of the building's layout led to the development of two separate card catalogs‍—‌the "Union" catalog and the "Public" catalog‍—‌housed on different floors and having a complex interrelationship "which perplexed students and faculty alike." It was not until the 1990s that the electronic <a href="/w/index.php?title=Harvard_On-Line_Library_Information_System&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Harvard On-Line Library Information System (page does not exist)">Harvard On-Line Library Information System</a> was able to completely supplant both physical catalogs.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:137,192</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Departmental_and_special_libraries">Departmental and special libraries</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Departmental and special libraries">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:242px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardCollegeLibrary_CatalogCardC.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bb/HarvardCollegeLibrary_CatalogCardC.jpg/240px-HarvardCollegeLibrary_CatalogCardC.jpg" width="240" height="94" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bb/HarvardCollegeLibrary_CatalogCardC.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="360" data-file-height="141" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardCollegeLibrary_CatalogCardC.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Library_catalog" title="Library catalog">Catalog card</a>. In the "Harvard system", <i>C</i> denotes Church History and Theology.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The building also houses a number of special libraries in dedicated spaces outside the stacks, includ­ing:</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 23em; -webkit-column-width: 23em; column-width: 23em;">
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Fred_N._Robinson" class="mw-redirect" title="Fred N. Robinson">Fred N. Robinson</a> <a href="/wiki/Celtic_studies" title="Celtic studies">Celtic</a> Seminar Library</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Hamilton_A.R._Gibb" class="mw-redirect" title="Hamilton A.R. Gibb">Hamilton A.R. Gibb</a> <a href="/wiki/Islamic_studies" title="Islamic studies">Islamic</a> Seminar Library</li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Milman_Parry" title="Milman Parry">Milman Parry</a> Collection of <a href="/wiki/Oral_Literature" class="mw-redirect" title="Oral Literature">Oral Literature</a></li>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Herbert_Weir_Smyth" title="Herbert Weir Smyth">Herbert Weir Smyth</a> <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Classical</a> Library</span></li>
</ul>
</div>
<ul>
<li>The <a href="/wiki/Francis_James_Child" title="Francis James Child">Francis James Child</a> Memorial Library <span class="nowrap">(English Department)</span></li>
<li>The <a href="/w/index.php?title=James_A._Notopoulos&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="James A. Notopoulos (page does not exist)">James A. Notopoulos</a> Collection of <span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/Modern_Greek" title="Modern Greek">Modern Greek</a> Ballads and Songs</span></li>
</ul>
<p>There are also special collections in the <a href="/wiki/History_of_science" title="History of science">history of science</a>, <a href="/wiki/Linguistics" title="Linguistics">linguis­tics</a>, <a href="/wiki/Near_East" title="Near East">Near Eastern</a> languag­es and civiliza­tions, <a href="/wiki/Palaeography" title="Palaeography">paleogra­phy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Sanskrit" title="Sanskrit">Sanskrit</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-special_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-special-119">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>The contents of the Treasure Room, holding Harvard's most precious rare books and manuscripts (other than the Harry Elkins Widener Collection itself) were transferred to newly built <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Library" title="Houghton Library">Houghton Library</a> in 1942.<sup id="cite_ref-houghton_chron_108-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton_chron-108">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:15</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_literature_and_legend">In literature and legend</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: In literature and legend">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span id="Swim-requirement,_ice-cream,_and_other_legends"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="Swim-requirement.2C_ice-cream.2C_and_other_legends">Swim-requirement, ice-cream, and other legends</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Swim-requirement, ice-cream, and other legends">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4.jpg" title="File:WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction 1913Dec4.jpg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg/330px-WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg" width="330" height="227" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ef/WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg/495px-WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/e/ef/WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="659" data-file-height="454" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:WidenerLibraryUnderConstruction_1913Dec4_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The stacks (here under con­struc­tion) double as struc­tur­al ele­ments,<sup id="cite_ref-snead_ad_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snead_ad-72">[63]</a></sup> mak­ing Wide­ner the last major self-support­ing mason­ry build­ing, with no outer steel frame, built in the US.<sup id="cite_ref-AZ_43-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:362</sup> The exterior walls are three feet thick.<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:316</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Legend holds that to spare future Harvard men her son's fate, Eleanor Widener insisted, as a condition of her gift, that learning to swim be made a requirement for graduation.<sup id="cite_ref-legends_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legends-120">[103]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-mooney_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mooney-121">[104]</a></sup> (This requirement, the <i><a href="/wiki/The_Harvard_Crimson" title="The Harvard Crimson">Harvard Crimson</a></i> once elaborated erroneously, was "dropped in the late 1970s because it was deemed discriminatory against physically disabled students".)<sup id="cite_ref-bibliophobia_68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibliophobia-68">[60]</a></sup> "Among the many myths relating to Harry Elkins Widener, this is the most prevalent", says Harvard's "Ask a Librarian" service. Though Harvard has had swimming requirements at various times (e.g. for <a href="/wiki/Crew_(sport)" class="mw-redirect" title="Crew (sport)">rowers</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Charles_River" title="Charles River">Charles River</a>, or as part of a now-defunct freshmen training regimen)<sup id="cite_ref-ask_swim_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_swim-122">[105]</a></sup> Bentinck-Smith writes that "There is absolutely no evidence in the President's papers, or the faculty's, to indicate that [Eleanor Widener] was, as a result of the Titanic disaster, in any way responsi­ble for [any] compulsory swimming test."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup></p>
<p>Another story, holding that Eleanor Widener donated a further sum to underwrite perpetual availability of ice cream (purportedly Harry Widener's favorite dessert) in Harvard dining halls, is also without foundation.<sup id="cite_ref-legends_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-legends-120">[103]</a></sup> A Widener curator's compilation of "fanciful oral history" recited by student tour guides includes "Flowers mysteriously appear every morning outside the Widener Room" and "Harry used to have <a href="/wiki/Carnation" class="mw-redirect" title="Carnation">carnations</a> dyed crimson to remind him of Harvard, and so his mother kept up the tradition" in the flowers displayed in the Memorial Rooms.<sup id="cite_ref-lies_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lies-123">[106]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Literary_references">Literary references</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Literary references">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/H._P._Lovecraft" title="H. P. Lovecraft">H.&#160;P. Lovecraft</a>'s fictional universe <a href="/wiki/Cthulhu_Mythos" title="Cthulhu Mythos">Cthulhu Mythos</a>, a 17th-century edition of the <i><a href="/wiki/Necronomicon" title="Necronomicon">Necro­nom­i­con</a></i> is hidden somewhere in the Widener stacks.<sup id="cite_ref-necro_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-necro-124">[107]</a></sup></p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Thomas_Wolfe" title="Thomas Wolfe">Thomas Wolfe</a>, who earned a Harvard <a href="/wiki/Master%27s_degree" title="Master's degree">master's degree</a> in 1922,<sup id="cite_ref-ask_wolfe_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_wolfe-125">[108]</a></sup> told <a href="/wiki/Max_Perkins" class="mw-redirect" title="Max Perkins">Max Perkins</a> that he spent most of his Harvard years in Widener's reading room.<sup id="cite_ref-shandtucci_31-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shandtucci-31">[28]</a></sup> He wrote of <span class="nowrap">"[wandering]</span> through the stacks of that great library like some damned soul, never at rest‍—‌ever leaping ahead from the pages I read to thoughts of those I want to read";<sup id="cite_ref-crime_wolfe_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_wolfe-126">[109]</a></sup> his <a href="/wiki/Alter_ego" title="Alter ego">alter ego</a> <a href="/wiki/Eugene_Gant" class="mw-redirect" title="Eugene Gant">Eugene Gant</a> read with a watch in his hand, "laying waste of the shelves."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-look_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-look-127">[110]</a></sup></p>
<p>Historian <a href="/wiki/Barbara_Tuchman" class="mw-redirect" title="Barbara Tuchman">Barbara Tuchman</a> considered "the single most formative experience" of her career the writing of her undergrad­uate thesis, for which she was "allowed to have as my own one of those little cubicles with a table under a window" in the Widener stacks, which were "my <a href="/wiki/Eureka_(word)#Archimedes" title="Eureka (word)">Archimedes' bathtub</a>, my <a href="/wiki/Burning_bush" title="Burning bush">burning bush</a>, my <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Fleming#accidental_discovery" title="Alexander Fleming">dish of mold</a> where I found my personal penicillin."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-tuchman_4-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tuchman-4">[4]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Burglary_and_other_incidents">Burglary and other incidents</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Burglary and other incidents">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Over the years, Widener has been the scene of various criminal exploits "infamous for their <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/feckless" class="extiw" title="wikt:feckless">fecklessness</a> and <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/ignominy" class="extiw" title="wikt:ignominy">ignominity</a>."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:59</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Joel_C._Williams"><span id="williams"></span>Joel C. Williams</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Joel C. Williams">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:182px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg/180px-HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg" width="180" height="102" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg/270px-HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/78/HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg/360px-HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg 2x" data-file-width="434" data-file-height="246" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardCollegeLibrary_HardLaborBookplate.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Bookplate" title="Bookplate">Bookplate</a> placed in 2504 books<sup id="cite_ref-crime_dont_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_dont-128">[111]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-bookplates_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bookplates-129">[112]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1931 former graduate student Joel C. Williams was arrested after attempting to sell two books bearing Harvard College Library stamps to a <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Square" title="Harvard Square">Harvard Square</a> book dealer, after which (the <i>Harvard Crimson</i> reported) "<a href="/wiki/Charles_R._Apted" title="Charles R. Apted">C.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>R. Apted</a>, Superin­tend­ent of Caretakers, together with officials of the Library, made a trip to Williams' home",<sup id="cite_ref-crime_indict_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_indict-130">[113]</a></sup> where (posing as "book buyers" to spare the feelings of Williams' family)<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:88</sup> they found thousands<sup id="cite_ref-crime_indict_130-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_indict-130">[113]</a></sup> of books stolen over the prior eight to ten years,<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:D</sup> many badly damaged, in barrels and wastebaskets. The "absolutely crazy" Williams would "go to students studying in Widener and ask them what course they were taking. He would then borrow all the books for that course in the library. Then no one could get any to study," library official John E. Shea later recalled.<sup id="cite_ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132">[r]</a></sup></p>
<p>Despite the misleading<sup id="cite_ref-ask_disturbing_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_disturbing-133">[115]</a></sup> implication of bookplates placed in the 2504<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:D</sup> recovered books, Harvard's charges against Williams were dropped after he was indicted on book-theft charges in another jurisdic­tion, which imposed a sentence of hard labor.<sup id="cite_ref-thieves_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-thieves-134">[116]</a></sup> After the unrelated arrest of a book-theft ring operating at Harvard, there was a "noticeable increase in the number of missing books secretly returned to the library", the <i>Transcript</i> reported in 1932.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:89</sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gutenberg_Bible_theft">Gutenberg Bible theft</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Gutenberg Bible theft">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg/330px-HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg" width="330" height="249" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg/495px-HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg/660px-HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1164" data-file-height="879" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarryElkinsWidener_Letter_1912March10_p2_final_paragraphs.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
"Now I will tell you a secret&#160;... I wish it was for me but it is not." Harry Wide­ner's letter con­fid­ing his grand­father's pur­chase of the <a href="/wiki/Gutenberg_bible" class="mw-redirect" title="Gutenberg bible">Gutenberg bible</a> later given to Harvard.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the night of August 19, 1969 an attempt was made to steal the library's Gutenberg Bible, valued at $1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million<sup id="cite_ref-suspect_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspect-135">[117]</a></sup> (equivalent to $11 million in 2016).<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US-NGDPPC_32-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-NGDPPC-32">[29]</a></sup> Equipped with a hammer, pry bar, and other burglarious implements, the 20-year-old would-be thief<sup id="cite_ref-suspect_135-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspect-135">[117]</a></sup> hid in a lavatory until after closing, then made his way to the roof, from which he descended via a knotted rope to break through a Memorial Room window. But after smashing the bible's display case and placing its two volumes in a knapsack, he found it impossible to reclimb the rope carrying his 70-pound (32<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>kg) booty.<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:D</sup></p>
<p>Eventually he fell some 50 feet (15&#160;m)<sup id="cite_ref-houghton_chron_108-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton_chron-108">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:45</sup> to the pavement of one of the light courts, where (despite landing on the knapsack)<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:D</sup> he lay semicon­scious<sup id="cite_ref-slips_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slips-136">[118]</a></sup> until his moans were heard by a janitor.<sup id="cite_ref-houghton_chron_108-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-houghton_chron-108">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:45</sup> He was found about 1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>a.m.,<sup id="cite_ref-grace_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grace-137">[119]</a></sup> "consid­er­a­bly the worse for his adventure",<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:D</sup> with injuries including a fractured skull.<sup id="cite_ref-slips_136-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slips-136">[118]</a></sup> "It looks like a profes­sion­al job all right, in the fact that he came down the rope," commented Harvard Police Chief <a href="/w/index.php?title=Robert_Tonis&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Robert Tonis (page does not exist)">Robert Tonis</a>. "But it doesn't look very profes­sion­al that he fell off."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-suspect_135-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspect-135">[117]</a></sup> Tonis specu­lated that the attempt may have been modeled on a similar caper depicted in the 1964 film<span style="white-space:nowrap"> </span><i><a href="/wiki/Topkapi_(film)" title="Topkapi (film)">Topkapi</a></i>,<sup id="cite_ref-grace_137-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-grace-137">[119]</a></sup> though a retired Harvard librarian later commented that the thief (who was later judged insane)<sup id="cite_ref-caper_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-caper-138">[120]</a></sup> "evidently knew nothing about books‍—‌or, at least, about selling them<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... There was no explanation of what he expected to do with the Bible."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:72</sup></p>
<p>Only the books' bindings (which were "not valuable [and] did just what a good binding is supposed to do: they protected the inside contents")<sup id="cite_ref-suspect_135-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-suspect-135">[117]</a></sup> were damaged.<sup id="cite_ref-slips_136-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slips-136">[118]</a></sup> Since the incident only one or the other Bible volume is on display at any given time<sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:E</sup> and a replica has been substituted at times of heightened security concern.<sup id="cite_ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140">[s]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span id="&quot;The_Slasher&quot;"></span><span class="mw-headline" id=".22The_Slasher.22">"The Slasher"</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: &quot;The Slasher&quot;">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Around 1990, empty bindings stripped of their pages began to appear in the Widener stacks. Eventually some 600 mutilated books were discovered, the vandal preferring works on early Christianity in Greek, Latin, or unusual languages such as <a href="/wiki/Icelandic_language" title="Icelandic language">Icelandic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-destroyer_94-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-destroyer-94">[80]</a></sup> Notes left at Widener, and later at <a href="/wiki/Northeastern_University" title="Northeastern University">Northeastern University</a>, threatened graphically described mutilations of library workers, cyanide gas attacks,<sup id="cite_ref-slasher_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slasher-141">[122]</a></sup> and bombings of libraries and a local bank.<sup id="cite_ref-calder_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-calder-142">[123]</a></sup> Other notes instructed that $500,000 be left in a Northeastern library, demanded that Northeastern "terminate all Jew personnel", and directed that $1<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million be left in the Widener stacks: "pUt THe mONEy FucKer BEhiNd THE eLevATOR on D<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>WEST in THE basemENT WhERE tHe 1,000,000.00 dollaRS IN rare GreEK bOOks wAS slASHEd ApARt <a href="/wiki/Jacques_Paul_Migne" title="Jacques Paul Migne">MIGNE</a> <a href="/wiki/Patrologia_Graeca" title="Patrologia Graeca">GREEK PATROLOGIA</a>." These "ransom drops" were staked out by the <a href="/wiki/FBI" class="mw-redirect" title="FBI">FBI</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-slasher_141-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slasher-141">[122]</a></sup> and surveillance cameras installed in ersatz books, without result.<sup id="cite_ref-without_result_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-without_result-143">[124]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 1994 police connected an incident at Northeastern, in which a library worker there (a former Widener employee) was caught stealing chemistry books, with the fact that chemistry texts had been among the works mutilated at Widener.<sup id="cite_ref-destroyer_94-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-destroyer-94">[80]</a></sup> Officials found "a kind of renegade reference room" in the worker's basement,<sup id="cite_ref-zoll_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-zoll-144">[125]</a></sup> includ­ing library books, piles of ripped-out pages, a <a href="/wiki/Microfilm" class="mw-redirect" title="Microfilm">microfilm</a> camera, and hundreds of unusable microfilms he had haphaz­ardly made of the books (worth $180,000) he had destroyed.<sup id="cite_ref-destroyer_94-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-destroyer-94">[80]</a></sup> At trial "The Slasher" said he had acted in revenge for the eighteen months he had been detained in a state <a href="/wiki/Psychiatric_hospital" title="Psychiatric hospital">psychiatric hospital</a> after expiration of a six-month jail term he had received for a minor offense.<sup id="cite_ref-slasher_141-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-slasher-141">[122]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Artwork">Artwork</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Artwork">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG/150px-Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG" width="150" height="200" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG/225px-Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG/300px-Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG 2x" data-file-width="2448" data-file-height="3264" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gore_Hall_finial_-_Harvard_University_-_IMG_0106.JPG" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
One of the two pin­na­cles, sal­vaged from <a href="/wiki/Gore_Hall_(Harvard_College_library)" title="Gore Hall (Harvard College library)">Gore Hall</a>, which now flank Widener's rear entrance</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Two of Gore Hall's granite pinnacles were preserved, and flank Widener's rear (Massachusetts Avenue) entrance.<sup id="cite_ref-shandtucci_31-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-shandtucci-31">[28]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:151</sup></p>
<p>In the 1920s the university commissioned <a href="/wiki/John_Singer_Sargent" title="John Singer Sargent">John Singer Sargent</a> to paint, within the fourteen-foot-high arched panels flanking the entrance to the Memorial Rotunda, two murals giving tribute to the Harvard's <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>I</a> dead: "Death and Victory" and "Entering the War".<sup id="cite_ref-146" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-146">[t]</a></sup> The accompanying inscription, by Lowell, reads: "Happy those who with a glowing faith<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>/ In one embrace clasped Death and Victory".<sup id="cite_ref-smith_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-smith-147">[127]</a></sup> With <a href="/wiki/Memorial_Church_of_Harvard_University" title="Memorial Church of Harvard University">Memorial Church</a>, which directly faces Widener, these constitute what the Boston Public Library calls "the most elaborate World War<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>I memorial in the Boston area."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-bpl_sargent_41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bpl_sargent-41">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>Above the Rotunda entrance is inscribed:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>To the memory of Eleanor Elkins Rice<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>&#160;•  whose noble and endearing spirit inspired the conception and completion of this Memorial Library<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>&#160;•  1938.<sup id="cite_ref-ask_mrsw_inscription_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_mrsw_inscription-148">[128]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<p>(Eleanor Elkins Widener became Eleanor Elkins Rice when, in October 1915, she married Harvard professor<sup id="cite_ref-weds_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-weds-149">[129]</a></sup> and surgeon<sup id="cite_ref-class1898_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-class1898-150">[130]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Alexander_H._Rice,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander H. Rice, Jr.">Alexander Hamilton Rice, Jr.</a>, a noted South American explorer whom she had met at the library's dedication four months earlier.<sup id="cite_ref-rotunda_62-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rotunda-62">[55]</a></sup> She died in 1937.)<sup id="cite_ref-dies_paris_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dies_paris-22">[20]</a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece.jpg" title="File:HarvardUniversity WidenerLibrary 1920 withArtilleryPiece.jpg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg/330px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg" width="330" height="208" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg/495px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/d9/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="637" data-file-height="401" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_1920_withArtilleryPiece_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Large bronze plaque in memory of Gore Hall is at lower right, just left of tree, in this 1920 photo. World War<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>I artillery piece was used by Department of Military Science and Tactics.<sup id="cite_ref-artillery_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-artillery-151">[131]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On the second floor is a bronze bust, by <a href="/wiki/Albin_Polasek" title="Albin Polasek">Albin Polasek</a>, of sculptor and muralist <a href="/wiki/Francis_Davis_Millet" title="Francis Davis Millet">Frank Millet</a>, who had also died on the <i>Titanic</i>.<sup id="cite_ref-bust_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bust-152">[132]</a></sup> In the main reading room is a sculpture of <a href="/wiki/George_Washington" title="George Washington">George Washington</a>; on the stairs to the third floor a sculpture of <a href="/wiki/John_Elbridge_Hudson" title="John Elbridge Hudson">John Elbridge Hudson</a>; and on the ground floor a sculpture of <a href="/wiki/Henry_Ware_Wales" class="mw-redirect" title="Henry Ware Wales">Henry Ware Wales</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-dienstag_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dienstag-153">[133]</a></sup> as well as vaulted hallways‍—‌"just like the <a href="/wiki/Oyster_bar" title="Oyster bar">Oyster Bar</a> at <a href="/wiki/Grand_Central_Terminal" title="Grand Central Terminal">Grand Central</a><span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... astounding", according to historian Thomas Gick‍—‌by <a href="/wiki/Rafael_Guastavino" title="Rafael Guastavino">Rafael Guastavino</a>, who (with his son) also designed and built domes and vaults in buildings such as <a href="/wiki/Carnegie_Hall" title="Carnegie Hall">Carnegie Hall</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Cathedral_of_St._John_the_Divine" class="mw-redirect" title="Cathedral of St. John the Divine">Cathedral of St. John the Divine</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Public_Library" title="Boston Public Library">Boston Public Library</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-lies_123-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lies-123">[106]</a></sup></p>
<p>Three dioramas‍—‌depicting the grounds, buildings, and vicinity of Harvard Yard in 1667, 1775 and 1936‍—‌were installed behind the main stairs in 1947, but removed during renovations in 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-ask_dioramas_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_dioramas-154">[134]</a></sup> A six-foot-square bronze tablet, featuring a <a href="/wiki/Bas_relief" class="mw-redirect" title="Bas relief">bas relief</a> of Gore Hall, is at the exterior northwest corner. Its inscription reads in part:</p>
<blockquote class="templatequote">
<p>On this spot stood Gore Hall &#160;• &#160;Architect <a href="/wiki/Richard_Bond_(architect)" title="Richard Bond (architect)">Richard Bond</a>, Supervisor &#160;• &#160;<a href="/wiki/Daniel_Treadwell" title="Daniel Treadwell">Daniel Treadwell</a> &#160;• &#160;Built in the <span class="nowrap">Year 1838</span> &#160;• &#160;In honor of <a href="/wiki/Christopher_Gore" title="Christopher Gore">Christopher Gore</a> Class of 1776. &#160;• &#160;<a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Harvard_College" title="President and Fellows of Harvard College">Fellow of the College</a>, <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Board_of_Overseers" title="Harvard Board of Overseers">Overseer</a>, Benefactor &#160;• &#160;<a href="/wiki/Governor_of_Massachusetts" title="Governor of Massachusetts">Governor of the Commonwealth</a>. &#160;• &#160;Senator of the United States. &#160;• &#160;The first use of <a href="/wiki/Bookstack" class="mw-redirect" title="Bookstack">modern book-stacks</a> was in this library. ... <sup id="cite_ref-gore_tablet_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gore_tablet-155">[135]</a></sup></p>
</blockquote>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Restrictions_on_women">Restrictions on women</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Restrictions on women">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915.jpg" title="File:HarvardUniversity WidenerLibrary Reading c1915.jpg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg/330px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg" width="330" height="204" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg/495px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f8/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg/660px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2311" data-file-height="1431" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_Reading_c1915_cropped.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The main reading room in 1915. By World War<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>II women could enter "to use the en­cy­clo­pe­dias and things like that there, if we stood up, but we couldn't sit down."<sup id="cite_ref-colson_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colson-156">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:56-7</sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The building originally included a separate <a href="/wiki/Radcliffe_College" title="Radcliffe College">Radcliffe</a> Reading Room behind the card catalogs‍—‌"barely large enough for a single table"‍—‌to which female students were restricted "for fear their presence would distract the studious Harvard men" in the Main Reading Room. In 1923 a sequence of communications between Librarian <a href="/wiki/William_Coolidge_Lane" title="William Coolidge Lane">William Coolidge Lane</a> and another Harvard official dealt with "the incident of Miss Alexander's intrusion into the reading room",<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:37,86</sup> and <a href="/wiki/Keyes_Metcalf" title="Keyes Metcalf">Keyes Metcalf</a>, Director of University Libraries from 1937 to 1955, wrote that early in his tenure a <a href="/wiki/Classics" title="Classics">Classics</a> professor "rushed into my office, looking as if he were about to have an apoplectic stroke, and gasped, 'I've just been in the reading room, and there is a Radcliffe girl in there!<span style="padding-right:.2em;">'</span>" By then female graduate students were permitted to enter the stacks, but only until 5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>p.m., "after which time it was thought they would not be safe there."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-157" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-157">[u]</a></sup></p>
<p>"Even the ever-present problem of inadequate lavatories worked to deny functional access to women", wrote Battles. "Patrons requesting directions to a women's restroom were routinely misled, denied access, or simply told that such things did not exist at a college for men such as Harvard."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:115</sup></p>
<p>By World War<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>II (<a href="/wiki/Elizabeth_Colson" title="Elizabeth Colson">Elizabeth Colson</a> recalled years later) "we could go into the [Main Reading Room] and use the encyclopedias and things like that there, if we stood up, but we couldn't sit down",<sup id="cite_ref-colson_156-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-colson-156">[136]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:56-7</sup> and only by special permission (which even female faculty members had to request in writing) could a woman work in the building in the evening.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:112-4</sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Renovation">Renovation</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Renovation">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:332px;"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915.jpeg" title="File:HarvardUniversity WidenerLibrary MassachusettsAvenueEntrance c1915.jpeg"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg/330px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg" width="330" height="155" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg/495px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/25/HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg/660px-HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="2555" data-file-height="1202" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:HarvardUniversity_WidenerLibrary_MassachusettsAvenueEntrance_c1915_cropped.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Southeast view of rear (<a href="/wiki/Massachusetts_Avenue_(metropolitan_Boston)" title="Massachusetts Avenue (metropolitan Boston)">Massa­chu­setts Ave.</a>) facade <span class="nowrap">c. 1915,</span> before construction of <a href="/wiki/Houghton_Library" title="Houghton Library">Hough­ton Library</a> and <a href="/wiki/Wigglesworth_Hall" class="mw-redirect" title="Wigglesworth Hall">Wiggles­worth Hall</a> to the east and south</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A five-year, $97<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million renovation completed in 2004<sup id="cite_ref-potier_50-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potier-50">[44]</a></sup> (the first since the building opened}<sup id="cite_ref-offsite_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-offsite-158">[137]</a></sup> added fire suppression and environ­men­tal control systems, upgraded wiring and communica­tions, remodeled various public spaces, and enclosed the light courts to create additional reading rooms<sup id="cite_ref-potier_50-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potier-50">[44]</a></sup> (beneath which several levels of new offices and mechanical equipment were hidden).<sup id="cite_ref-bigdoings_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bigdoings-159">[138]</a></sup> "Claustro­pho­bia-inducing" elevators were replaced,<sup id="cite_ref-gewertz_102-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gewertz-102">[87]</a></sup> the bottom shelves on the lowest stacks level were removed in recognition of chronic seepage problems,<sup id="cite_ref-offsite_158-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-offsite-158">[137]</a></sup> Widener's "olfactory nostal­gia<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... actually the smell of decaying books" was addressed,<sup id="cite_ref-goins_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-goins-160">[139]</a></sup> and unrestricted light and air‍—‌seen as desirable when Widener was built but now considered "public enemies one and two for the long-term safety of old books"‍—‌were brought under control.<sup id="cite_ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161">[v]</a></sup></p>
<p>Some changes required that the Widener family grant relief<sup id="cite_ref-wilkinson_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wilkinson-162">[140]</a></sup> from the terms of Eleanor Widener's gift, which forbade that "structures of any kind [be] erected in the courts around which the [Library] is constructed, but that the same shall be kept open for light and air".<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1976_15-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1976-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:79</sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:42</sup> The need to relocate each of the building's 3.5<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>million volumes twice‍—‌first to temporary locations, then back, as work proceeded aisle by aisle‍—‌was turned to advantage, so that by the end of the renova­tion related materials in the library's two classifica­tion systems (see <a href="#Parallel_classification_systems_and_dual_catalogs">§ Parallel classifica­tion systems</a>) were physically adjacent for the first time;<sup id="cite_ref-movement_118-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-movement-118">[101]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-gewertz_102-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-gewertz-102">[87]</a></sup> the chart showing the floor and wing location, within the stacks, of each subject classifica­tion was revised sixty-five times during construction.<sup id="cite_ref-potier_50-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potier-50">[44]</a></sup> The project received the 2005 Library Building Award from the <a href="/wiki/American_Library_Association" title="American Library Association">American Library Associa­tion</a> and the <a href="/wiki/American_Institute_of_Architects" title="American Institute of Architects">American Institute of Architects</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ala_aia_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ala_aia-163">[141]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="See_also">See also</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: See also">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

<li><a href="/wiki/Harvard_Bixi" title="Harvard Bixi">Harvard Bixi</a></li>
</ul>
