<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Widener_Library&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Notes">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="reflist columns references-column-width" style="-moz-column-width: 30em; -webkit-column-width: 30em; column-width: 30em; list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-9"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-9">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-ask_inscriptions_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_inscriptions-7">[7]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-transcript_8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-transcript-8">[8]</a></sup> The quotation "He labored not for himself only<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..." alludes to <a href="/wiki/Ecclesiasticus" class="mw-redirect" title="Ecclesiasticus">Ecclesi­as­ti­cus</a> 33:17.</span></li>
<li id="cite_note-20"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-20">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-canoe_18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canoe-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:88</sup> "When I cease to be President of Harvard College," Lowell wrote around this time, "I shall join one of the <a href="/wiki/Mendicant_orders" title="Mendicant orders">mendicant orders</a>, so as to have less begging to do<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:23</sup> In May 1911 the <i><a href="/wiki/Boston_American" title="Boston American">Boston American</a></i> (published by disgraced Harvard dropout <a href="/wiki/William_Randolph_Hearst" title="William Randolph Hearst">William Randolph Hearst</a>)<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-hearst_19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hearst-19">[18]</a></sup> carried a mock adver­tise­ment: "Wanted‍—‌a millionaire. Will some kind millionaire please give Harvard Univer­sity a library building? Tainted money not barred. <a href="/wiki/John_D._Rockefeller" title="John D. Rockefeller">Mr. Rockefeller</a>, take notice. <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Carnegie" title="Andrew Carnegie">Mr. Carnegie</a>, please write."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-canoe_18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canoe-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:87</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-26"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-26">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Stipulations on conditions of storage began to appear in bequests to Harvard's libraries during the nineteenth century.<sup id="cite_ref-burke_24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-burke-24">[22]</a></sup> For example, the 1883–84 annual report of <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Divinity_School" title="Harvard Divinity School">Harvard Divinity School</a>'s dean noted that <a href="/wiki/Ezra_Abbot" title="Ezra Abbot">Ezra Abbot</a><span class="nowrap" style="padding-left:0.1em;">'</span>s widow, in donating four thousand volumes from his personal library, asked for assurance that a better and safer replacement for the existing Divinity School library building be constructed promptly; the dean also wrote that such a replacement would encourage future donors.<sup id="cite_ref-divinity_25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-divinity-25">[23]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-34"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-34">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:14</sup><sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US-NGDPPC_32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-NGDPPC-32">[29]</a></sup> Eleanor Widener expressed vexation at newspapers' misreporting of the circumstances of her gift, writing to Lowell, "I want emphasized<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... that the library is a memorial to my dear son, to be known as the 'Harry Elkins Widener Memorial Library,' given by me<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>&amp; not his [paternal grandfather <a href="/wiki/P._A._B._Widener" class="mw-redirect" title="P. A. B. Widener"><span class="nowrap">P. A. B.</span> Widener</a>] as has been so often stated."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-mrsw_33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-mrsw-33">[30]</a></sup> Years later her second husband <a href="/wiki/Alexander_Hamilton_Rice,_Jr." class="mw-redirect" title="Alexander Hamilton Rice, Jr.">A.<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>H. Rice, Jr.</a> insisted that Lowell do his best "to see that in all official reports, etc. the Library is referred to as the Harry Elkins Widener Memorial Library‍—‌Widener! Not one cent of Widener money, one second of Widener thought, nor one ounce of Widener energy were expended on either the conception or construction of the Library."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:15</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-37"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-37">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-meister_35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-meister-35">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:147</sup> Eleanor Widener was not similarly honored, because women were ineligible for Harvard honorary degrees at the time.<sup id="cite_ref-observed_17-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-observed-17">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:72</sup> The <i><a href="/w/index.php?title=Harvard_Graduates_Magazine&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Harvard Graduates Magazine (page does not exist)">Harvard Graduates Magazine</a></i> reassured its readers that the admission of ladies, for the first time, to certain Commencement proceedings "will not, however, create any precedent. It was due to the dedication of the Library, which demanded that once, at least, custom should be broken in favor of Mrs. Widener and her friends<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-sanders_36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sanders-36">[32]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-39"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-39">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-canoe_18-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canoe-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:89</sup> <i>The Library Journal</i> commented: "The building has administrative disadvantages necessitate by its character as a memorial, with a central <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/fane#English-temple" class="extiw" title="wikt:fane">fane</a> housing the private library collected by young Widener<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... This occupies what woiuld otherwise be the central court and cuts off access from the stack except at the two ends, but is scarcely to be criticized in view of the splendor of the gift and the parental affection thus enshrined and perpetuated by Mrs. Widener."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-tlj_may1915_38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tlj_may1915-38">[33]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-59"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-59">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-ask_furniture_55-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_furniture-55">[49]</a></sup> The rug is a <a href="/wiki/Heriz_rug" title="Heriz rug">Heriz Persian</a>;<sup id="cite_ref-ask_rug_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_rug-56">[50]</a></sup> on the desk is an unsigned <a href="/wiki/Tiffany_lamp" title="Tiffany lamp">Tiffany lamp</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-ask_lamp_57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_lamp-57">[51]</a></sup> In the library's early years, when the Memorial Rooms served as the office of the Widener Collec­tion's curator, fires were sometimes set in the fireplace.<sup id="cite_ref-whitehill_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-whitehill-58">[52]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-65"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-65">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-opens_64-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-opens-64">[57]</a></sup> Trumbauer "had no rivals when it came to tempting clients to spend immodest sums", wrote Wayne Andrews,<sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1980_11-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1980-11">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:16</sup> and Biel wrote that he had "made his name and fortune by knowing that 'only a magnifi­cent setting could hope to satisfy an American with a magnifi­cent income,' and he had already imparted such magnifi­cence to the Widener and Elkins mansions and an assortment of other palaces<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... [He] knew who his client was, so he gave elaborate attention to memorial­izing Harry in style" in the Memorial Rooms.<sup id="cite_ref-canoe_18-7" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-canoe-18">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:89</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-69"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-69">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-bibliophobia_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bibliophobia-68">[60]</a></sup> In March 1916 Eleanor Widener wrote to <a href="/wiki/George_Parker_Winship" title="George Parker Winship">George Parker Winship</a>, curator of the Widener Collection (who at the time used the Memorial Room as his office): "Will you please see that at all times fresh flowers are kept on your table by the photograph of my dear son Harry, the same to be paid out of funds set aside for the maintenance of the Memorial Room. This is the only request I make, and I beg of you to see that it is always carried out."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:43</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-75"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-75">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-snead_6-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-snead-6">[6]</a></sup> In the basement (later converted to additional shelving as stacks levels C and D)<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-crime_1928_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_1928-73">[64]</a></sup> were</span>
<dl>
<dd>the <a href="/wiki/Dynamo" title="Dynamo">dynamos</a> which run the five elevators and two book-lifts, the compressed air machinery for the pneumatic tubes, the dynamo and fan for the vacuum-cleaning system, a pump connected with the steam-heating apparatus, enormous fans which pump warm air into the Reading-Room and the stack, a filter through which passes all water which enters the building, and the connec­tions for electric light and power. The building is to be heated by steam, conveyed through a tunnel from the plant of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Elevated_Railway" title="Boston Elevated Railway">Elevated Railroad Company</a>, which also furnishes heat to the other buildings of the <a href="/wiki/Harvard_Yard" title="Harvard Yard">College Yard</a> and to the freshman dormitories.<sup id="cite_ref-lane_mem_71-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-lane_mem-71">[62]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:328</sup></dd>
</dl>
<div class="paragraphbreak" style="margin-top:0.5em"></div>
<p>The marble floors were polished using a machine "so simple that any laborer of ordinary intelli­gence can operate it to advantage [yet it] can do the work of ten men rubbing by hand."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-engineering_74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-engineering-74">[65]</a></sup></p>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-77"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-77">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"The [faculty studies] are not all fully used," Coolidge wrote in 1917, "but you will understand that I can not go to a professor and tell him that I think he is not making use of his space and had better give it up. I have tried in some cases hinting to people that if they did not need their quarters there were others who could make good use of them. These hints have usually met with conspicuously little success."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:72-75</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-79"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-79">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"At present", Carney continued, "everyone using the stack is obliged to go to the basement to reach the public toilet. This in the case of a man using one of the top floors of the stack is a particularly long trip<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... An emergency toilet<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... would be a desirable thing."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:59</sup> By 1937 security changes had made the situation even worse, so that someone on the lowest stack level had to climb seven flights of stairs, exit the stack, then descend another set of stairs to reach the basement toilet. Eventually toilets were installed in the stack by Harvard Librarian Keyes Metcalf, who later wrote that "As far as graduate students are concerned, I will go down in history as the man who provided toilet facilities in the Widener stack."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:139–40</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-85"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-85">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:27</sup> On any given floor of the stack, it is 400 feet (120&#160;m) from the entrance to the furthest shelves, and a patron "concerned with material in widely different fields may find that a tiresome amount of walking and stair climbing is involved."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:91,74</sup> English professor <a href="/wiki/Howard_Mumford_Jones" title="Howard Mumford Jones">Howard Mumford Jones</a> complained in 1950 that in preparing a lecture on <a href="/wiki/Robert_Frost" title="Robert Frost">Robert Frost</a>, after a long hunt for a bibliography listing works he would need to consult, then locating those works in the complicated catalogs, he found that</span>
<dl>
<dd><i>the American Scholar</i> is shelved on Floor A; the <i>New English Quarterly</i> under New England; the <i>Classical Journal</i> is shelved on Floor 5; and <i>College English</i> is in Educ on Floor B. I shall not go into the matter of distribution [of these works among wings] East, South, and West&#160;...<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:133-4</sup></dd>
</dl>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-91"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-91">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-collections_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-collections-3">[3]</a></sup> However, "Harvard does not collect all subjects and all types of material<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... The holdings in subject areas not represented in the curriculum (such as agriculture) are understandably limited<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-stam_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-stam-89">[76]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:352</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-98"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-98">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-destroyer_94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-destroyer-94">[80]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-klepts_95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-klepts-95">[81]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:E</sup> It was not always so. Originally "school-boys" earning forty dollars per month (equivalent to $480 in 2016)<sup id="cite_ref-inflation-US_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-inflation-US-96">[82]</a></sup> fetched books requested via slips submitted to the Delivery Room. "Should a slip be received for a book in a part of the stack where a boy has just been sent‍—‌particularly in the West stack, which is the farthest away from the central station‍—‌the [request] is telephoned across on the internal telephone."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:56</sup> But by about 1930 Widener's stacks "were almost wide open to anyone who wanted to enter", so much so that in a single day a group of thieves was able to steal some one hundred valuable works on American history.<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1980_97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1980-97">[83]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-107"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-107">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-halberstam_66-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-halberstam-66">[58]</a></sup> The December<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>31, 1912 agreement between Eleanor Widener and the <a href="/wiki/President_and_Fellows_of_Harvard_College" title="President and Fellows of Harvard College">President and Fellows of Harvard College</a> provides that "this collection, together with such books as may be added to it by members of the family of the Donor, shall at all times be kept separate and apart from the general library of Harvard<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... Harvard is not<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... ever to add anything to the said Harry Elkins Widener collection<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... [S]aid books shall not be taken or removed from the two rooms specially set apart<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>... excepting only when necessary for the repair or restoration of any volume<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>..."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-bentinck1976_15-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bentinck1976-15">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:78-9</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-114"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-114">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-ask_gutenberg_111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_gutenberg-111">[95]</a></sup> Harry Widener knew his grandfather had bought the Gutenberg Bible, but not that it was intended for him. "I wish it was for me but it is not", he wrote to a friend.<sup id="cite_ref-HEW_Livingston_112-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HEW_Livingston-112">[96]</a></sup> After Harry's death, and (soon after) that of his grandfather, the Bible passed to Harry's uncle;<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="which uncle? (May 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> at the uncle's death Harry's brother and sister added the Bible to the Harry Elkins Widener Collec­tion because it "had been bought for Harry and should be among his books." <a href="/wiki/Yale_University" title="Yale University">Yale</a> also has a Gutenberg, though not in "quite as fine condition" as Harvard's, according to Harvard officials.<sup id="cite_ref-near_best_113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-near_best-113">[97]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-132"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-132">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-crime_1951_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_1951-131">[114]</a></sup> <span id="shea"></span>John Shea was for forty years Widener's "guardian and familiar spirit". His mother had been a college "<a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/biddy#English-irishmaid" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:biddy">biddy</a>" who (he said) "did professor <a href="/wiki/Charles_Townsend_Copeland" title="Charles Townsend Copeland">C.&#160;T. Copeland's</a> laundry for years",<sup id="cite_ref-crime_1951_131-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_1951-131">[114]</a></sup> and he began his own Harvard career in 1905 as a Gore Hall coatchecker. By his 1954 retirement as Widener's Stacks Superin­tendent, he was "perhaps the last of the legendary College characters",<sup id="cite_ref-AZ_43-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AZ-43">[37]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:58</sup> renowned not only for leaving "no stone unthrown"‍—‌as he himself put it‍—‌in locating mis-shelved or otherwise errant books, but also for his "genius for such malaprop­isms [which] in fact, were generally the <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/mot_juste#Noun" class="extiw" title="wiktionary:mot juste">mot juste</a></i>." These included references to "venereal blinds" and "osculating fans" in the Catalog Room, equipment that had "outlived its uselessness", a gift of a bottle of wine "as a momentum", and mention that Widener's head janitor "has a maniac for sweeping the basement."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-sheavian_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-sheavian-1">[1]</a></sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-140"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-140">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-crime_1969_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-crime_1969-139">[121]</a></sup> Extensive news coverage of the attempt triggered a flurry of inquiries to Harvard about the potential value of family bibles and Gutenberg-related bric-a-brac.<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:146-7</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-146"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-146">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-ask_sargent_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ask_sargent-145">[126]</a></sup> Eleanor Widener "had originally stipulated that no further memorials would be permitted within her library, but the war had softened her feelings on the matter. Too many Harvard men died in the conflict to ignore their loss‍—‌and further, it seems, Eleanor came to connect Harry's death with their sacrifice." (Battles)<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:63</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-157"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-157">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">After his retirement Metcalf wrote that when planning the later <a href="/wiki/Lamont_Library" title="Lamont Library">Lamont Library</a>, "I was still old fashioned enough enough to believe that, if women [would permitted to use it] we should probably not have the small, unsupervised reading rooms that we were planning."<span class="Unicode nowrap"> </span><sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:87</sup></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-161"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-161">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><sup id="cite_ref-potier_50-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-potier-50">[44]</a></sup> "Before the renovation, the upper [stacks] floors smelled, in summer, of gently roasted books, while [the lowest floor] year-round offered the <a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/sporiferous" class="extiw" title="wikt:sporiferous">sporiferous</a> scent usually associated with grottoes and Roman cellars." (Battles)<sup class="reference nowrap"><a href="#B">[B]</a>:180</sup></span>
<div class="paragraphbreak" style="margin-top:0.5em"></div>
<p><span class="reference-text">When Widener was built ventilation for books was emphasized, possibly to prevent mold; thus a slit ran along the base of every row of shelves, allowing air to flow from the floor below. Unfortunately books, papers and objects were prone to fall through these slits,<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1965_76-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1965-76">[66]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:135</sup> and "the whole installation might have been regarded as a large collection of chimneys that would help a fire to spread rapidly from floor to floor." The slits were later closed.<sup id="cite_ref-metcalf1988_78-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-metcalf1988-78">[67]</a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap">:92-3</sup></span></p>
</li>
</ol>
</div>

<li id="cite_note-hewc-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-hewc_5-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-hewc_5-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Harvard College Library (2009). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://hcl.harvard.edu/libraries/houghton/collections/widener/collection.cfm">"Harry Elkins Widener Collec­tion. <li id="cite_note-snead-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink">^ <a href="#cite_ref-snead_6-0"><sup><i><b>a</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-snead_6-1"><sup><i><b>b</b></i></sup></a> <a href="#cite_ref-snead_6-2"><sup><i><b>c</b></i></sup></a></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation book"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://books.google.com/books?id=Kr9XAAAAMAAJ&amp;pg=PA153"><i>Library planning, bookstacks and shelving, with contri­bu­tions from the archi­tects' and librarians' points of view</i></a>. Snead<span class="nowrap">&#160;</span>&amp; Company Iron Works. 1915. pp.&#160;11, 68, 152–8.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWidener+Library&amp;rft.btitle=Library+planning%2C+bookstacks+and+shelving%2C+with+contri%26shy%3Bbu%26shy%3Btions+from+the+archi%26shy%3Btects%27+and+librarians%27+points+of+view&amp;rft.date=1915&amp;rft.genre=book&amp;rft.pages=11%2C+68%2C+152-8&amp;rft.pub=Snead%3Cspan+class%3D%22nowrap%22%3E+%3C%2Fspan%3E%26+Company+Iron+Works&amp;rft_id=https%3A%2F%2Fbooks.google.com%2Fbooks%3Fid%3DKr9XAAAAMAAJ%26pg%3DPA153&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Abook" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
<li id="cite_note-ask_inscriptions-7"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-ask_inscriptions_7-0">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text"><cite class="citation web">Kelley-Milburn, Deborah (March 25, 2013). <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://asklib.hcl.harvard.edu/a.php?qid=380877">"What are the inscriptions to Harry by his mother in the entrance to the memorial library at Harvard?"</a>. <i>Harvard Library. Ask a Librarian</i><span class="reference-accessdate">. Retrieved <span class="nowrap">June 14,</span> 2014</span>.</cite><span title="ctx_ver=Z39.88-2004&amp;rfr_id=info%3Asid%2Fen.wikipedia.org%3AWidener+Library&amp;rft.atitle=What+are+the+inscriptions+to+Harry+by+his+mother+in+the+entrance+to+the+memorial+library+at+Harvard%3F&amp;rft.aufirst=Deborah&amp;rft.aulast=Kelley-Milburn&amp;rft.date=2013-03-25&amp;rft.genre=unknown&amp;rft.jtitle=Harvard+Library.+Ask+a+Librarian&amp;rft_id=http%3A%2F%2Fasklib.hcl.harvard.edu%2Fa.php%3Fqid%3D380877&amp;rft_val_fmt=info%3Aofi%2Ffmt%3Akev%3Amtx%3Ajournal" class="Z3988"><span style="display:none;">&#160;</span></span></span></li>
